I enable **incremental checkpoints** on RocksDB so only the changed **SST files** are uploaded to the checkpoint store ‚Äî not the full state. On large stateful jobs, that cuts checkpoint size dramatically.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "When do you switch off the **HashMap** backend?" **A ‚Äî** "Past a few gigabytes ‚Äî it's bound by **heap size**, so I move to **RocksDB**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why **RocksDB**?" **A ‚Äî** "It spills to local **SSD** and holds **terabytes** of state per task manager."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How does **checkpointing** give exactly-once?" **A ‚Äî** "It snapshots all **operator state** and source **offsets** together, so a restart **replays** cleanly."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How do you set the **checkpoint interval**?" **A ‚Äî** "By recovery budget ‚Äî a **thirty-second** interval means at most thirty seconds of **replay**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do **incremental checkpoints** save?" **A ‚Äî** "Only the changed **SST files** upload, which cuts checkpoint **size** dramatically."

### Kafka ‚Äî KRaft, durability, and tiered storage

**Kafka 4.0** ships with **KRaft** as the only supported mode ‚Äî **ZooKeeper** is fully removed. KRaft embeds the **metadata quorum** inside the Kafka brokers themselves. That removes the ZooKeeper cluster entirely, which reduces operational complexity and speeds up **controller failover** from seconds to milliseconds. For **durability**, the three settings that matter together are `acks=all`, `min.insync.replicas`, and `replication.factor`. Set any one of them incorrectly and it undermines the others. I always set `min.insync.replicas` to at least two, so a single **broker** failure doesn't silently reduce acknowledgment to just one replica. **Tiered storage** lets brokers offload **cold segments** to object storage like S3, which decouples retention policy from broker disk capacity. I can keep six months of history in a topic at near-zero marginal cost. The trade-off is slightly higher **fetch latency** for consumers reading old offsets. I handle that by keeping the last few hours of segments on local disk.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What changed in **Kafka 4.0**?" **A ‚Äî** "**KRaft** is the only mode now ‚Äî **ZooKeeper** is fully removed."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why is **KRaft** better operationally?" **A ‚Äî** "The **metadata quorum** lives in the brokers, so **controller failover** drops from seconds to **milliseconds**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Which three **durability** settings matter together?" **A ‚Äî** "`acks=all`, `min.insync.replicas`, and `replication.factor` ‚Äî one wrong **undermines** the others."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why set `min.insync.replicas` to **two**?" **A ‚Äî** "So a single **broker** failure doesn't silently drop acknowledgment to **one** replica."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **trade-off** with tiered storage?" **A ‚Äî** "Slightly higher **fetch latency** on **cold** offsets ‚Äî I keep recent **segments** on local disk."

### Data engineering ‚Äî idempotent pipelines and the medallion architecture

The hardest operational problem in data engineering isn't writing the pipeline ‚Äî it's making it safe to **re-run**. An **idempotent** pipeline produces exactly the same output whether it runs once or ten times. That means retries after failures never corrupt the target table. I achieve this by writing to a **staging partition** keyed on the processing date, then doing an **atomic swap** or overwrite of that partition. Partial writes from a failed run are simply overwritten on the next attempt. The **medallion architecture** gives me three clear **trust boundaries**: **Bronze** holds raw, immutable ingestion records; **Silver** applies schema enforcement, deduplication, and **data quality** assertions; **Gold** holds business-level aggregates ready for dashboards and models. I gate promotion from Silver to Gold with automated checks ‚Äî **null rates**, **row counts** against expected ranges, and **referential integrity** spot-checks. For **change data capture** pipelines, I use **Debezium** reading the **WAL** to emit fine-grained row events to Kafka. That lets downstream consumers rebuild any point-in-time snapshot without polling the source database. Keeping pipelines **replayable** by a date-range parameter means a **backfill** is just a re-run with earlier bounds ‚Äî no special-case code path needed.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **hardest** part of a pipeline?" **A ‚Äî** "Not writing it ‚Äî making it safe to **re-run** so retries never **corrupt** the table."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How do you make a write **idempotent**?" **A ‚Äî** "I write to a **staging partition** by date, then do an **atomic swap** or overwrite."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do the **medallion** layers enforce?" **A ‚Äî** "**Bronze** is raw, **silver** adds schema and **dedup**, gold holds business **aggregates**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How do you **gate** promotion to gold?" **A ‚Äî** "Automated checks ‚Äî **null rates**, **row counts**, and **referential integrity** spot-checks."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How do you run a **backfill**?" **A ‚Äî** "It's just a **re-run** with an earlier **date range** ‚Äî no special-case code path."

### Platform ‚Äî Kubernetes resource management and JVM sizing

Running **JVM** workloads on **Kubernetes** requires care around **memory** sizing. By default, the JVM reads total **host memory** rather than the **cgroup limit** of the container. That means it can allocate a **heap** far larger than the **pod** is allowed to use, and the kernel kills the process with an **OOM kill**. Starting with **Java 17**, the JVM is **container-aware** by default and reads the cgroup limit correctly. I set `-XX:MaxRAMPercentage` to around seventy-five percent. That leaves room for **off-heap** allocations, **metaspace**, and thread stacks. I also always set **resource requests** equal to **resource limits** for JVM pods. That gives them a **Guaranteed** QoS class, so the **kubelet** won't evict them under memory pressure. And I wire **readiness probes** to a lightweight health endpoint that checks whether the **JVM** has finished **JIT** compilation warm-up ‚Äî only then does the pod start receiving production traffic.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the classic **JVM**-on-Kubernetes trap?" **A ‚Äî** "The JVM reads **host memory**, not the **cgroup limit**, so the **heap** triggers an **OOM kill**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "When did that get **fixed**?" **A ‚Äî** "**Java 17** ‚Äî the JVM is **container-aware** by default and reads the cgroup correctly."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Where do you set the **heap**?" **A ‚Äî** "**`-XX:MaxRAMPercentage`** around seventy-five percent, leaving room for **off-heap** and metaspace."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why **requests equal limits** for JVM pods?" **A ‚Äî** "It gives a **Guaranteed** QoS class, so the **kubelet** won't evict under memory pressure."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do your **readiness probes** check?" **A ‚Äî** "Whether the JVM finished **JIT** warm-up ‚Äî only then does the pod take **production** traffic."

### Platform ‚Äî Observability and error budgets

My **observability** stack is built on **OpenTelemetry** for all three signals ‚Äî **metrics**, **logs**, and **traces** ‚Äî with a single **collector** pipeline that fans out to the right backends. For reliability targets, I prefer **SLO**-based alerting over raw **error-rate** thresholds. The reason is that an **error budget** gives you a concrete policy for when to slow down **deployments**. The key alert I configure is a **burn-rate** alert. It fires when the team is consuming the budget at a rate that would exhaust it before the **rolling window** ends ‚Äî which is far more **actionable** than a static threshold. I apply the **RED method** ‚Äî **request rate**, **error rate**, **duration** ‚Äî for every **service**. And I apply the **USE method** ‚Äî **utilization**, **saturation**, **errors** ‚Äî for every **infrastructure resource**. I also keep **label cardinality** under control by never putting high-cardinality values like user IDs into **metric labels**. Those belong in **trace attributes** instead.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "When would you NOT use a **static** error-rate **threshold**?" **A ‚Äî** "Almost always ‚Äî I prefer a **burn-rate** alert, because a flat threshold pages you on noise and stays quiet on a slow **leak**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "A junior asks what an **error budget** is ‚Äî what do you say?" **A ‚Äî** "It's the **failures** you're allowed in the window. If you've burned it, you **stop shipping** and fix reliability first."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What goes wrong with high **cardinality** labels?" **A ‚Äî** "I once put **user IDs** in a **metric label** and blew up the **time series** count. User IDs belong in **trace attributes**, not labels."
- **Q ‚Äî** "When do you reach for **RED** versus **USE**?" **A ‚Äî** "**RED** for every **service** ‚Äî rate, errors, **duration**. **USE** for the **hardware** ‚Äî utilization, **saturation**, errors."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why one **OpenTelemetry** collector instead of three agents?" **A ‚Äî** "One **collector pipeline** for **metrics**, **logs**, and **traces** means one place to sample, **redact**, and fan out to each backend."

### Platform ‚Äî Security and supply-chain hygiene

My approach to **supply-chain security** follows a layered model. At **build time**, I generate an **SBOM** and **sign** the **container image** with **Sigstore Cosign** using **keyless** authentication via **OIDC**. That means there's no long-lived **signing key** to manage. At **deploy time**, an **admission controller** verifies the **signature** before allowing the image to run in the cluster. For **secrets**, I never commit them to **Git**. I use a **secrets manager** and inject values at runtime either as **environment variables** or **mounted files**. For **service-to-service authentication**, I use **mTLS** with **SPIFFE SVID** identities issued by **SPIRE**. That removes the need for any shared **API key**. And every workload runs with a **minimal privilege** profile: **non-root** user, **read-only root filesystem**, and **capabilities** dropped to only what the process actually requires.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "Why **keyless** signing instead of a key?" **A ‚Äî** "With **Sigstore Cosign** and **OIDC** there's no long-lived **signing key** to leak or **rotate** ‚Äî the identity is short-lived."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What stops an **unsigned** image from running?" **A ‚Äî** "An **admission controller** verifies the **signature** at **deploy time** and just refuses to start the pod if it doesn't match."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How do you handle **secrets**?" **A ‚Äî** "Never in **Git** ‚Äî a **secrets manager** injects them at runtime as **env vars** or **mounted files**, so they never sit in the image."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why **mTLS** with **SPIFFE** over a shared **API key**?" **A ‚Äî** "Each workload gets its own **SVID** identity from **SPIRE**, so there's no shared **secret** that, once stolen, unlocks everything."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's your default **container** hardening?" **A ‚Äî** "**Non-root** user, **read-only root filesystem**, and **capabilities** dropped to only what the process truly needs."

### Behavioral ‚Äî biggest technical challenge

The hardest technical problem I faced was a **data loss** bug in a high-throughput **streaming pipeline**. The tricky part was that it only showed up under sustained **backpressure**, so I could never reproduce it in staging. What I did was instrument the **consumer lag** and **offset commit** timing with **distributed traces**. Eventually that led me to it. A **rebalance** event was re-queuing some messages, but the downstream writer had no **idempotency** check, so those messages just vanished. I fixed the root cause, added a **reconciliation job** to detect and re-process any gaps, and then wrote a **chaos test** that deliberately triggers rebalances under load. That way the regression can't come back silently. What I took away was this: subtle **concurrency** bugs need instrumentation, not just unit tests. And the fix has to be paired with something that keeps verifying correctness over time.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "It only showed up under sustained **backpressure**, so I could never **reproduce** it in staging ‚Äî that's what made it brutal."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you finally **find** it?" **A ‚Äî** "I instrumented **consumer lag** and **offset commit** timing with **traces**, and a **rebalance** turned out to be re-queuing messages with no **idempotency** check."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently**?" **A ‚Äî** "I'd add the **chaos test** that triggers rebalances first, before the bug ‚Äî not after it cost us **data**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** that it was actually fixed?" **A ‚Äî** "The **reconciliation job** stopped finding gaps, and the chaos test ran clean under load on every build."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you **watch** for next time?" **A ‚Äî** "Any **concurrency** path that isn't covered by **instrumentation** ‚Äî unit tests never catch these; only **production-shaped** load does."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a conflict with a teammate

A senior colleague and I disagreed on how to **partition** a new **event stream**. He wanted a flat single-partition design for simplicity. I thought we needed **keyed partitions** to guarantee **ordering** per entity. Instead of just debating it back and forth, I suggested we each write a one-page **design doc** with our assumptions and **trade-offs**. Then we'd share them async and hold a thirty-minute review with a third engineer for a **tie-break opinion**. That format made it easy to keep the technical argument separate from any personal friction. In the end we landed on a hybrid: keyed partitions with a **compaction policy** ‚Äî something neither of us had suggested to begin with. What I learned is that structured **design reviews** are more productive than real-time debates when both people have strong opinions. I use that format by default now for any meaningful **architectural** decision.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part of that disagreement?" **A ‚Äî** "Keeping it about the **partitioning** and not about ego ‚Äî we both had strong opinions and it could've gotten personal."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **teammate** react to your proposal?" **A ‚Äî** "He was relieved, honestly ‚Äî writing a one-page **design doc** each took the heat out of arguing in real time."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **break the tie**?" **A ‚Äî** "A third engineer gave a **tie-break opinion**, and that's actually where the **hybrid** keyed-plus-**compaction** idea came from."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently**?" **A ‚Äî** "Nothing major ‚Äî but I'd write the docs **before** the first heated meeting, not after."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you do **differently** now because of it?" **A ‚Äî** "I default to a structured **design review** for any real **architectural** call instead of debating live."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a failure and what I learned

I once pushed a **configuration change** to a production **Kafka** cluster without going through the **change management** process. I was confident it was low-risk and I wanted to move fast. But that change triggered a cascade of **consumer group** rebalances that delayed a critical **reporting pipeline** by about forty minutes and woke up two on-call engineers. In the **post-mortem** I wrote, I was honest that I had bypassed the process ‚Äî not that the process had failed. We used the incident to tighten the **rollout policy** and add an automatic **dry-run** step for cluster-level changes. The lesson I carry from it is that **confidence** in a change is not a reason to skip **oversight**. The process exists exactly for the cases where your confidence is wrong. I haven't bypassed a **change review** since, and now I'm usually the one asking others whether they filed the ticket.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part to own?" **A ‚Äî** "Writing in the **post-mortem** that I **bypassed** the process ‚Äî not that the process failed. That was on me."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How bad was the **blast radius**?" **A ‚Äî** "A cascade of **consumer-group** rebalances delayed a **reporting pipeline** about forty minutes and woke two **on-call** engineers."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently**?" **A ‚Äî** "File the **change ticket** even when I'm sure it's low-risk ‚Äî **confidence** is exactly when I'm most likely to be wrong."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **team** react to your post-mortem?" **A ‚Äî** "Well, because it was **blameless** on the process and honest about me ‚Äî we used it to add an automatic **dry-run** step."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you **watch** for now?" **A ‚Äî** "I'm now the one asking others if they've filed the **change review** ‚Äî I haven't skipped one since."

### Behavioral ‚Äî disagreeing with my tech lead about a technical decision

So we were building a new ingestion service, and my tech lead wanted to use **Kafka Streams** for the whole thing ‚Äî that's what he knew well. But I'd looked at our use case, and we needed to join against a slow-changing dimension table in **Postgres**. To me that felt much cleaner in **Flink** with its async I/O. I didn't want to say "you're wrong" in front of everyone, so after the meeting I went to him one-on-one. I said, "Hey, I might be missing something ‚Äî can you walk me through how you'd do the enrichment in **Kafka Streams**?" And as he explained it, he started to see that the join was going to be painful. So I showed him a small prototype I'd already built in **Flink** over the weekend. In the end we went with **Flink**. What I learned is, the respectful way to disagree is to ask honest questions first, not to walk in already sure you're right.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "Disagreeing with my **tech lead** without making it look like I was calling him wrong in front of the team."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **approach** him?" **A ‚Äî** "One-on-one, and I asked him to walk me through the **enrichment** in **Kafka Streams** ‚Äî as he explained, he saw the **join** was painful."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** that **Flink** was the right call?" **A ‚Äî** "I'd built a small weekend **prototype** with **async I/O** against the **Postgres** dimension table, so we could see it work, not just argue it."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did he **react**?" **A ‚Äî** "He came around himself ‚Äî because I asked **questions** instead of pushing slides, it never felt like a fight."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently**?" **A ‚Äî** "I'd surface the **trade-off** earlier, before the group meeting, so we don't decide twice."

### Behavioral ‚Äî when I disagreed but committed fully anyway

At one point the team decided to move our batch jobs from a self-managed **Spark** cluster onto a managed cloud service. I was against it, honestly. I thought we'd lose fine control over **JVM** tuning and the cost would go up. I made my case in the **design doc** review and put real numbers on the table. But the team and the manager still felt the operational savings were worth it. Once that decision was made, I chose not to be the person dragging his feet. So I actually volunteered to lead the migration. I wrote the runbook, tuned the new cluster, and helped two other engineers move their jobs. And after a few months the **on-call** load really did drop, and I was a bit wrong about the cost too. What I learned is, once a decision is made, the team needs me rowing in the same direction ‚Äî even if I wasn't the one who picked it.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "Choosing to fully **commit** to a plan I'd argued against ‚Äî it's tempting to quietly **drag your feet**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **show** you committed?" **A ‚Äî** "I volunteered to **lead the migration** ‚Äî wrote the **runbook**, tuned the new cluster, moved two engineers' jobs myself."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the outcome?" **A ‚Äî** "**On-call** load really did drop over a few months, and the **cost** came in better than I'd predicted ‚Äî I was a bit wrong."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did the **team** think of your turnaround?" **A ‚Äî** "It built trust ‚Äî they saw I'd **row in the same direction** even when my hand didn't pick the plan."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently**?" **A ‚Äî** "Put my **cost numbers** to a quick test before the **design-doc** review ‚Äî I overestimated the downside."

### Behavioral ‚Äî pushing back on an unrealistic deadline

So a product manager came to us wanting a brand-new real-time dashboard, fed from **Kafka** through a fresh **Flink** pipeline into **Trino**, and he wanted it live in two weeks for a big customer demo. I knew two weeks for all of that, production-grade, wasn't real. We'd skip testing and it would fall over during the demo ‚Äî the worst possible time. So I didn't just say no. I sat with him and split the work into pieces. I said, "In two weeks I can give you a read-only version on yesterday's data, refreshed hourly, and it'll look almost the same in the demo. The true real-time part I need about five more weeks to do safely." He actually liked that, because he mostly needed it to look good for the customer. In the end the demo went fine on the hourly version, and we shipped real-time later without rushing. What I learned is, pushing back works much better when you bring an option instead of just a problem.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "Saying the two-week **deadline** wasn't real without sounding like I was just refusing to do the work."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **push back**?" **A ‚Äî** "Not a flat no ‚Äî I split it. A read-only **hourly** version in two weeks, real-time over **Kafka**, **Flink**, **Trino** in five more."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **PM** react?" **A ‚Äî** "He liked it, because he mostly needed it to **look good** for the customer **demo**, not be fully real-time on day one."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** it worked out?" **A ‚Äî** "The **demo** went fine on the hourly build, and we shipped real-time later without **rushing** or skipping tests."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently**?" **A ‚Äî** "Bring the **phased option** to the very first conversation ‚Äî pushing back lands better with a plan than a problem."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a code-review disagreement where I changed my own mind

So a teammate opened a pull request that added a big **caching** layer in front of our metadata service. My first reaction was that it was over-engineering. I left a comment saying we should just add a database index and keep it simple. He pushed back politely and asked me to look at the actual traffic. So I went and pulled the real numbers from our **observability** dashboards. It turned out this service was getting hammered ‚Äî the same few keys read thousands of times a second. An index alone wasn't going to fix that kind of **read amplification**. So I was wrong. I went back on the pull request and wrote, "You're right, I missed the read pattern ‚Äî the cache makes sense. Let's just add a metric for the hit rate." We merged it. What I learned is, in code review I should check the data before I plant my flag. And it's totally fine ‚Äî even good ‚Äî to change my mind in writing where the team can see it.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "Admitting in the **pull request**, in writing, that my first take was wrong ‚Äî my reflex was 'this is **over-engineering**.'"
- **Q ‚Äî** "What **changed** your mind?" **A ‚Äî** "I pulled the real traffic from our **observability** dashboards ‚Äî a few hot keys read thousands of times a second, pure **read amplification**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why wasn't an **index** enough?" **A ‚Äî** "An index doesn't fix the same key being read over and over ‚Äî only the **cache** kills that **amplification**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **teammate** react?" **A ‚Äî** "He appreciated it ‚Äî I wrote 'you're right, I missed the **read pattern**,' and we just added a **hit-rate** metric and merged."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you do **differently** now?" **A ‚Äî** "I check the **data** before I plant my flag in a review ‚Äî and I'm fine changing my mind where the team can see it."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a cross-team conflict over a shared cluster

Another team and mine shared the same **Kubernetes** cluster for our **Spark** jobs. Their big nightly job kept eating all the resources, and my pipelines would get starved and miss their **SLA**. The easy move would have been to complain loudly that they were hogging everything. But that just makes people defensive. So instead I gathered the facts first. I pulled the scheduling metrics and they clearly showed when the two jobs collided. Then I asked their lead for a coffee chat ‚Äî not a formal meeting ‚Äî and showed him the timeline. He honestly hadn't realized the overlap. Together we set up proper resource quotas, a separate **priority class**, and shifted their job to start an hour earlier. In the end both teams hit their SLAs and there were no hard feelings. What I learned is, when another team causes me pain, I should walk in with data and curiosity, not blame ‚Äî it keeps the relationship intact for next time.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "Resisting the urge to just **complain** loudly that they were hogging the **cluster** ‚Äî that only makes people defensive."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **open** the conversation?" **A ‚Äî** "I asked their lead for a **coffee chat**, not a formal meeting, and showed the scheduling **timeline** ‚Äî he hadn't realized the **overlap**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you actually **fix**?" **A ‚Äî** "Together we set **resource quotas**, a separate **priority class**, and shifted their **Spark** job an hour earlier."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** success?" **A ‚Äî** "Both teams hit their **SLAs** after that, and there were no **hard feelings** ‚Äî that part mattered to me."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you **watch** for next time?" **A ‚Äî** "I walk in with **data** and curiosity, not **blame** ‚Äî it keeps the relationship intact for the next conflict."

### Behavioral ‚Äî convincing a skeptical senior engineer

We had a slow batch job that reprocessed everything every night, and I thought we should move it to incremental processing using **Iceberg** with its hidden partitioning and snapshot reads. But one senior engineer was really skeptical. He'd been burned by table formats before and thought it'd be flaky and hard to debug. I didn't try to win it with slides. Instead I asked him for one afternoon to run a small proof of concept on one real table, with him watching. We ran it together, and I made sure to show the failure cases too ‚Äî what happens on a bad write, and how snapshots let us roll back. Seeing it recover cleanly is what turned him around. He went from "no way" to suggesting the next table we should migrate. In the end we cut that job's runtime by more than half. What I learned is, a skeptical senior doesn't want promises. He wants to see it work and see it fail safely, with his own eyes.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "He'd been **burned** by **table formats** before, so the skepticism was real ‚Äî slides were never going to move him."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **win** him over?" **A ‚Äî** "One afternoon, a small **proof of concept** on one real table, with him watching ‚Äî including the **failure cases**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why show the **failure** cases on purpose?" **A ‚Äî** "Because what turned him was seeing **Iceberg** **roll back** cleanly from a bad write using **snapshots** ‚Äî recovery, not promises."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the win?" **A ‚Äî** "We cut that job's **runtime** by more than half, and he went from 'no way' to suggesting the next table to **migrate**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **learn** for next time?" **A ‚Äî** "A skeptical senior wants to see it **work and fail safely** with his own eyes ‚Äî so I build the demo, not the pitch."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a production outage I caused with a heavy query

A few years back I was building a small dashboard that read from our main **PostgreSQL** database ‚Äî the same one our checkout service used. I wrote a query to count orders per region and it worked fine on my laptop with test data. But in production we had millions of rows, and I'd forgotten the column I was filtering on had no index. The moment I deployed, that query started doing a full **table scan** over and over and locked up the **connection pool**. Within about ten minutes checkout slowed down and orders started failing. My team lead pinged me, and honestly my stomach dropped. What I did first was kill the running query and then **roll back** my deploy ‚Äî stop the bleeding before anything else. After that I moved the dashboard to read from a **read replica** instead of the primary, and I added the missing index. The whole **blast radius** lasted about fifteen minutes. What I learned is, I never trust a query until I run `EXPLAIN` on it against real data volumes first.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "That gut-drop moment when checkout started **failing** and I realized my **dashboard** query was the cause."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What **broke**, exactly?" **A ‚Äî** "My query filtered on a column with **no index**, so it did a **full table scan** over millions of rows and locked the **connection pool**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you do **first**?" **A ‚Äî** "**Killed** the running query, then **rolled back** the deploy ‚Äî stop the bleeding before anything else."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you keep the **blast radius** small?" **A ‚Äî** "I moved the dashboard to a **read replica** off the primary and added the missing **index** ‚Äî the whole thing lasted about fifteen minutes."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you **watch** for now?" **A ‚Äî** "I never trust a query until I run **`EXPLAIN`** on it against real **data volumes** first."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a night on-call incident

One night around 3 a.m. my phone went off because **consumer lag** on one of our **Kafka** topics was climbing fast. I dragged myself to my laptop. The **Flink** job consuming that topic had crashed and was stuck in a restart loop. At that hour it was very tempting to just bounce everything and go back to bed. But I forced myself to actually read the logs first. It turned out one bad message with a corrupted field was making the job throw an exception, and it kept replaying the same message and dying again. So what I did was move the consumer offset past that one poison message so the job could move forward. Then I caught the lag back up over the next half hour. After that I opened a ticket to add proper error handling, so a single bad record goes to a **dead-letter** topic instead of killing the whole job. What I learned is, even at 3 a.m., read the logs first ‚Äî don't just restart and hope.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part at **3 a.m.**?" **A ‚Äî** "Fighting the urge to just **bounce** everything and go back to bed instead of reading the **logs**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did the **logs** show?" **A ‚Äî** "One **poison message** with a corrupted field made the **Flink** job throw, replay the same record, and die in a **restart loop**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **stop the bleeding**?" **A ‚Äî** "I moved the **consumer offset** past that one bad message so the job could move forward, then caught the **lag** back up over half an hour."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you do **differently** afterward?" **A ‚Äî** "Opened a ticket to route a single bad record to a **dead-letter** topic instead of letting it kill the whole job."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's your **rule** from that night?" **A ‚Äî** "Even half-asleep, **read the logs first** ‚Äî don't just **restart** and hope it goes away."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a bug I shipped that reached users

I once shipped a change to our reporting **Spark** job that calculated daily active users. I changed how we deduplicated user events and thought I'd made it more accurate. But a week later someone from the business side said the daily numbers looked about twenty percent too low ‚Äî and they were right. The bug was mine. My new dedup logic was dropping legitimate events that happened to share a timestamp. Nobody noticed for a few days because the drop looked gradual. What I did first was reprocess the affected days with the old logic so the dashboards showed correct numbers again. And I told the stakeholders exactly which days had been wrong. Then I fixed the dedup to key on the event ID instead of the timestamp. The important part: I added a **data quality** check that compares each day's count against a rolling average and alerts if it swings more than ten percent. What I learned is, a silent wrong number is worse than a loud crash ‚Äî so now I build the alarm that would've caught me.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest** part?" **A ‚Äî** "Owning it ‚Äî the **20%** drop was my new **dedup** logic, and it was **silent**, so nobody caught it for days."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What exactly went **wrong**?" **A ‚Äî** "I keyed dedup on the **timestamp**, so it dropped legitimate events that happened to share one ‚Äî in a daily-active-users **Spark** job."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you do **first** once you knew?" **A ‚Äî** "**Reprocessed** the affected days with the old logic so the dashboards were correct, and told stakeholders exactly which days were wrong."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the fix held?" **A ‚Äî** "I added a **data-quality** check comparing each day to a **rolling average** ‚Äî it alerts if the count swings more than **10%**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **learn**?" **A ‚Äî** "A silent **wrong number** is worse than a loud **crash** ‚Äî so now I build the **alarm** that would've caught me."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a deadline I missed

So I was given two weeks to migrate one of our pipelines to write into **Iceberg** tables instead of plain Parquet files. I thought it'd be tight but doable. About a week in, I hit a wall ‚Äî our older **Trino** version didn't fully support the **Iceberg** features I needed. So I had to coordinate a version upgrade first, and that pulled in the platform team. At that point it was clear I wasn't going to make the date. To be honest, my first instinct was to push harder and hope I'd catch up quietly. But I stopped myself. I messaged my manager that same day. I said it plainly: here's where I am, here's the blocker, and I think I need one more week. I also gave him a couple of options, like shipping a read-only version first. He was totally fine with it, because I gave him room to plan. In the end I delivered about a week late, but solid. What I learned is, bad news doesn't age well ‚Äî tell people early, while they can still react.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of that situation?" **A ‚Äî** "Honestly, **resisting** the urge to push harder and **hide** it ‚Äî telling my manager that same day felt risky."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently** next time?" **A ‚Äî** "I'd flag the **Trino** version risk in my first **estimate**, not a week in when it became a **blocker**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did your **manager** react when you told him?" **A ‚Äî** "He was totally **fine** with it, because I gave him **options** and enough room to **re-plan**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you make sure the late delivery was **solid**?" **A ‚Äî** "I shipped a **read-only** version first, then validated the **Iceberg** writes against the old **Parquet** output."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **lesson** you carry from it?" **A ‚Äî** "Bad news doesn't **age** well ‚Äî I tell people **early**, while they can still **react**."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a technical decision I reversed

So when we were designing a new event store, I pushed hard for **Cassandra**. My thinking was, we'd have huge write volume, and **Cassandra** handles writes really well. We built the first version on it. But after a couple of months, it became clear that the way our analysts actually queried the data was nothing like I'd assumed. They ran lots of ad-hoc filters across many fields, and **Cassandra** just isn't built for that. Every new access pattern meant designing a new table, and we were drowning. I had to admit my call was wrong. So instead of defending it, I put together a small benchmark comparing our **Cassandra** setup against **Iceberg** queried through **Trino** on the same data. The numbers made the case for me. We migrated over about a month, and the analysts could finally just write normal SQL. What I learned is, pick the database around how people will read the data, not just how fast you can write it ‚Äî and don't let pride keep a wrong decision alive.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of reversing your own call?" **A ‚Äî** "**Admitting** it out loud ‚Äî I'd pushed hard for **Cassandra**, so eating that was uncomfortable."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **convince** the team to switch?" **A ‚Äî** "I didn't **argue** ‚Äî I built a small **benchmark** of **Cassandra** versus **Iceberg** on **Trino**, and the numbers spoke."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** that the move was right?" **A ‚Äî** "The **analysts** could finally run normal **SQL** with **ad-hoc** filters, instead of a new **table** per access pattern."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Who **pushed back**, and how did you handle it?" **A ‚Äî** "A teammate who liked the **write** speed ‚Äî so I showed the **query** side was where we were actually **drowning**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **earlier** next time?" **A ‚Äî** "Ask the **analysts** how they'll **read** the data before I pick the **database**, not after."

### Behavioral ‚Äî an early-career mistake

So early in my career I was still pretty new, and I had access I probably shouldn't have had yet. I was cleaning up some old test data. I ran a `DELETE` statement directly against a production table. The problem was, I forgot the `WHERE` clause. I wiped out about a day's worth of real customer records before I even realized what had happened. My heart just sank. The first thing I did was not try to fix it alone ‚Äî I told my senior engineer right away, because I knew hiding it would only make things worse. Together we restored the table from the nightly backup, and we only lost a couple of hours of data that we could replay from **Kafka**. The recovery took maybe an hour. After that I started always writing the `WHERE` clause first and running a `SELECT` to see what I'd hit before I ever change it to `DELETE`. What I learned is two things: respect production, and own a mistake out loud the second it happens.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was going through your **head** the moment it happened?" **A ‚Äî** "My heart just **sank** ‚Äî I'd run a **`DELETE`** with no **`WHERE`** and wiped a day of real records."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **first thing** you did?" **A ‚Äî** "I didn't try to **fix** it alone ‚Äî I told my **senior** engineer right away, because hiding it makes it worse."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **recover** the data?" **A ‚Äî** "We restored from the **nightly backup**, and **replayed** the last couple of hours from **Kafka**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you make sure it **never** happened again?" **A ‚Äî** "Now I always write the **`WHERE`** first and run a **`SELECT`** to see what I'd hit before I touch **`DELETE`**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did the **mistake** teach you?" **A ‚Äî** "Two things ‚Äî **respect** production, and **own** a mistake out loud the second it happens."

### Behavioral ‚Äî leading a migration without being the official lead

So we had this really old **batch** ingestion job that everyone was scared to touch. It was slow, and it was breaking almost every week, but nobody owned it officially. I wasn't the lead ‚Äî just one of the engineers on the team ‚Äî but I got tired of the **on-call** pages. What I did was write a short one-page plan for moving it onto **Spark** with proper checkpointing. I shared it with the team and asked for honest feedback. I didn't wait for anyone to give me permission. I started with the smallest piece, got one pipeline migrated, and showed the numbers ‚Äî the run time dropped from about four hours to forty minutes. After people saw that, they actually wanted in. Three other engineers picked up the rest with me, and in the end we moved the whole thing in about two months. What I learned is, you can lead just by writing the plan down and shipping the first small win ‚Äî the **authority** follows the results.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you lead without any **authority**?" **A ‚Äî** "I wrote a short **one-page plan** for moving the job to **Spark**, shared it, and just **started** on the smallest piece."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of getting people on board?" **A ‚Äî** "Nobody **owned** the job, so at first it was just me ‚Äî I had to **prove** it was worth their time."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** that it was working?" **A ‚Äî** "One pipeline's run time dropped from about **four hours** to **forty minutes** ‚Äî that number sold it."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **team** respond once they saw that?" **A ‚Äî** "They actually **wanted in** ‚Äî three engineers picked up the rest, and we moved it all in about **two months**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **learn** about leading?" **A ‚Äî** "You can lead just by **writing the plan down** and shipping the first small win ‚Äî **authority** follows results."

### Behavioral ‚Äî mentoring a junior engineer

When a new grad joined our team, they got assigned to a **Kafka** consumer service. And honestly they were a bit lost ‚Äî they kept getting stuck but wouldn't ask for help, because they didn't want to look slow. So the first thing I did was set up a short thirty-minute pairing slot every morning, just to unblock them, no pressure. I told them very clearly: asking questions early is the smart move, not the weak one. And instead of giving them the answer, I'd ask "okay, where do you think the problem is?" ‚Äî so they learned to debug on their own. We worked through **consumer group** rebalances and **offset** handling together. After a few weeks, they fixed a tricky **lag** issue completely by themselves and even wrote a runbook for it. By the end of the quarter they were taking their own **on-call** shifts. What I learned is, mentoring is mostly about making it safe to be confused ‚Äî once people feel safe, they grow fast.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of mentoring them?" **A ‚Äî** "Getting them to **ask** early ‚Äî they kept getting **stuck** quietly because they didn't want to look slow."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you actually **structure** the help?" **A ‚Äî** "A short **thirty-minute** pairing slot every morning, just to **unblock** them, no pressure."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why not just **give** them the answer?" **A ‚Äî** "I'd ask 'where do you think the **problem** is?' so they learned to **debug** on their own."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** their growth?" **A ‚Äî** "After a few weeks they fixed a tricky **lag** issue alone, wrote a **runbook**, and took their own **on-call** shifts."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's your **takeaway** on mentoring?" **A ‚Äî** "It's mostly about making it **safe** to be confused ‚Äî once people feel safe, they grow **fast**."

### Behavioral ‚Äî driving the ZooKeeper to KRaft migration

We were running a few **Kafka** clusters still on **ZooKeeper**, and it was extra operational pain ‚Äî two systems to monitor, slower failovers, and **ZooKeeper** was getting deprecated. I pushed for moving to **KRaft** mode. The tricky part was that this touches production directly, so people were nervous. So the first thing I did was build a small test cluster and run the migration there, end to end. I wrote down every single step and everything that could go wrong. Then I shared that runbook with the team and we did a dry run together on staging. We picked the lowest-traffic cluster first, did the cutover in a maintenance window, and watched the metrics closely. It went clean. After that, the team trusted the process, and we did the rest one by one over about six weeks ‚Äî zero data loss. What I learned is, for a scary **migration**, you earn trust by going first on something small and writing everything down so it's repeatable.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What made this migration so **scary**?" **A ‚Äî** "It touches **production** directly, and we were moving **Kafka** off **ZooKeeper** onto **KRaft** ‚Äî people were nervous."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **first thing** you did?" **A ‚Äî** "I built a small **test cluster**, ran the migration end to end, and wrote down every **step** and every thing that could go wrong."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **earn** the team's trust?" **A ‚Äî** "I went **first** on something small ‚Äî a **dry run** on staging, then the lowest-traffic cluster in a **maintenance window**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** success?" **A ‚Äî** "Clean cutover, **zero data loss**, and we watched the **metrics** closely on every cluster as we went."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you tell someone facing a **risky** migration?" **A ‚Äî** "Go first on something **small** and write everything down so it's **repeatable** ‚Äî that's how you earn trust."

### Behavioral ‚Äî influencing a decision across teams that disagreed

We had three teams all writing data into the lake in different formats, and it was a mess ‚Äî the analytics team couldn't query things reliably. I thought we should standardize on **Iceberg** as the table format, but each team had their own opinion and nobody wanted to change their pipeline. And I had no power over those teams. So instead of arguing in a big meeting, I talked to each team lead one on one first, just to understand what they actually cared about. One cared about **schema** changes, one about query speed, one about cost. Then I put together a small proof of concept on **Iceberg** that showed it handled all three concerns, with real numbers on **Trino** query times. When we finally met as a group, I wasn't selling my idea ‚Äî I was showing how it solved each of their problems. They agreed. What I learned is, you win cross-team decisions by listening first and framing the answer around what each **stakeholder** needs, not what you want.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** with three teams disagreeing?" **A ‚Äî** "I had **no power** over them, and each one cared about something different ‚Äî **schema**, **speed**, or **cost**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you approach it instead of a big **meeting**?" **A ‚Äî** "I talked to each lead **one on one** first, just to understand what they actually **cared** about."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you make the case **concrete**?" **A ‚Äî** "A small **proof of concept** on **Iceberg** with real **Trino** query numbers that hit all three concerns."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **group** react when you finally met?" **A ‚Äî** "They **agreed** ‚Äî because I wasn't selling my idea, I was showing how it solved each of **their** problems."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **lesson** on cross-team influence?" **A ‚Äî** "You win by **listening** first and framing the answer around what each **stakeholder** needs, not what you want."

### Behavioral ‚Äî owning a messy problem nobody wanted

We had this **schema registry** situation that was a total mess ‚Äî different services using incompatible **Avro** schemas, and every few weeks a deploy would break a downstream consumer. Everyone agreed it was bad, but it sat across two teams so nobody owned it. And honestly, it wasn't fun work. I just decided to take it. First I spent a couple of days mapping out every producer and consumer and which schema version they used, because nobody had the full picture. That alone surfaced three time bombs waiting to break. Then I set up **schema compatibility** checks in **CI** so a breaking change gets caught before it ships, not in production. I also wrote clear rules for how to evolve a schema safely. After that, the breakages basically stopped ‚Äî we went from a few incidents a month to almost none. What I learned is, the messy unowned problems are usually the highest-value ones, and just raising your hand to own it is half the battle.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "Why did this **schema registry** problem sit unowned?" **A ‚Äî** "It spanned **two teams** and honestly wasn't fun work, so nobody **raised their hand** ‚Äî I just took it."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **first thing** you did?" **A ‚Äî** "I mapped every **producer** and **consumer** and their **Avro** version ‚Äî nobody had the full picture."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **hardest part** of that kind of cleanup?" **A ‚Äî** "Starting blind ‚Äî that mapping alone surfaced three **time bombs** waiting to break."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **stop** the breakages?" **A ‚Äî** "I added **schema compatibility** checks in **CI**, so a breaking change gets caught before it **ships**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the win?" **A ‚Äî** "We went from a few **incidents** a month to almost none ‚Äî the unowned mess was the highest-value work."

### Behavioral ‚Äî improving our on-call process

Our **on-call** was rough ‚Äî alerts were noisy, half of them weren't real, and people were getting woken up at night for things that could wait until morning. Folks were burning out and nobody wanted the pager. I wasn't the manager, but I asked if I could run a small review of our alerts. So I went through about three months of pages and sorted them: which ones were real incidents, which ones were noise, and which ones just had badly tuned thresholds. It turned out almost half were noise. I worked with the team to delete or re-tune those, and we split alerts into "wake me up now" versus "look at it tomorrow." I also started a quick fifteen-minute handoff at the end of each **on-call** week so the next person knew what was shaky. After a couple of months, night pages dropped by more than half. What I learned is, you can't fix on-call by working harder ‚Äî you fix it by looking at the data and cutting the noise.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of fixing **on-call**?" **A ‚Äî** "I wasn't the **manager**, so I first had to ask if I could even run a **review** of the alerts."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you find the real **problem**?" **A ‚Äî** "I went through **three months** of pages and sorted them ‚Äî real incidents, **noise**, and badly tuned **thresholds**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did the **data** actually show?" **A ‚Äî** "Almost **half** the pages were noise ‚Äî we deleted or re-tuned those right away."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **change** about the process?" **A ‚Äî** "We split alerts into 'wake me up **now**' versus 'look at it **tomorrow**,' plus a quick fifteen-minute **handoff**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** it worked?" **A ‚Äî** "Night **pages** dropped by more than **half** ‚Äî you fix on-call by cutting noise, not working harder."

### Behavioral ‚Äî the hardest bug I ever debugged

So the hardest bug I ever found was a slow **memory leak** in a **JVM** service that read from **Kafka** and wrote into a database. The service ran fine for about two days. Then it would get long **garbage collection** pauses, and in the end it would just die with an **out of memory** error. At first I thought it was a bad **heap** size, so I gave it more memory. But that only bought us one more day. So what I did was take a **heap dump** right before it crashed and open it in a tool. And there it was ‚Äî a static **cache** with no size limit. Every new message key added one more entry that never got removed. The map just grew forever. The fix itself was small: I put a bounded **LRU cache** in place, and the pauses went away. What I learned is, when something dies slowly over days, don't just add more **heap** ‚Äî take the dump and look at what's actually holding the memory.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What made this bug so **hard**?" **A ‚Äî** "It was a slow **memory leak** ‚Äî the **JVM** service ran fine for two days, then long **GC** pauses, then **out of memory**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was your **first** guess, and was it wrong?" **A ‚Äî** "I thought it was a small **heap**, so I added memory ‚Äî that only bought us **one more day**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you finally **find** the root cause?" **A ‚Äî** "I took a **heap dump** right before it crashed ‚Äî a static **cache** with no size limit, growing forever."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the actual **fix**?" **A ‚Äî** "Small ‚Äî I put in a bounded **LRU cache**, and the **pauses** went away completely."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **sooner** next time?" **A ‚Äî** "When something dies slowly over days, don't add **heap** ‚Äî take the **dump** and see what's holding memory."

### Behavioral ‚Äî delivering under a tight deadline

So one time we promised a customer a new **daily report** pipeline before a big launch, and I had about one week ‚Äî really tight. The thing is, the **Spark** job I wrote was correct but way too slow. It ran for almost five hours, and the report had to be ready by 6 a.m. every morning. That was not going to work. With the **deadline** so close I couldn't rewrite everything, so I focused only on the slowest part. I looked at the **Spark UI** and saw one huge **shuffle** caused by a join on a skewed key. That was the whole problem. So what I did was broadcast the small table instead of shuffling, and repartition the big one. That cut the job from almost five hours down to about forty minutes. We shipped it the night before the launch. What I learned is, under real pressure you don't fix everything ‚Äî you find the one bottleneck that matters and you hit that.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** under that deadline?" **A ‚Äî** "My **Spark** job was correct but ran almost **five hours**, and the report had to be ready by **6 a.m.**"
- **Q ‚Äî** "With one week, how did you **decide** what to fix?" **A ‚Äî** "I couldn't **rewrite** everything, so I focused only on the **slowest** part and ignored the rest."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you find the **bottleneck**?" **A ‚Äî** "The **Spark UI** showed one huge **shuffle** from a join on a **skewed** key ‚Äî that was the whole problem."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What exactly did you **change**?" **A ‚Äî** "I **broadcast** the small table instead of shuffling and **repartitioned** the big one ‚Äî five hours down to forty minutes."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **lesson** on tight deadlines?" **A ‚Äî** "Under real pressure you don't fix **everything** ‚Äî you find the one **bottleneck** that matters and hit that."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a big decision with incomplete information

So a while back we had to pick a **table format** for our new **data lake** ‚Äî basically **Iceberg** or **Delta** ‚Äî and honestly we didn't have full information. We didn't know yet how big the data would grow, or which query engines other teams would want to use later. But I couldn't wait six months to be sure ‚Äî people needed to start building. So instead of trying to find the perfect answer, I wrote down the things we were fairly sure about. We knew we'd use **Trino** and **Spark**, and we knew we wanted **schema evolution** and **time travel**. Those points pointed pretty clearly to **Iceberg**. I also made sure the decision wasn't a one-way door ‚Äî we kept the raw data in **Parquet** underneath, so we could re-table it later if we were wrong. In the end **Iceberg** worked out well. What I learned is, with **ambiguity** you decide on what you do know, and you protect yourself by keeping the door open.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of deciding then?" **A ‚Äî** "We didn't know how big the **data** would grow or which **engines** teams would want ‚Äî but people needed to **start**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you decide with that **ambiguity**?" **A ‚Äî** "I wrote down what we were **sure** of ‚Äî we'd use **Trino** and **Spark**, and wanted **schema evolution** and **time travel**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **protect** yourself if you were wrong?" **A ‚Äî** "I kept it a **two-way door** ‚Äî raw data stayed in **Parquet** underneath, so we could **re-table** later."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** that it was the right call?" **A ‚Äî** "**Iceberg** worked out ‚Äî the points we knew pointed clearly to it, and nothing forced us to **back out**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's your **takeaway** on incomplete info?" **A ‚Äî** "Decide on what you **do** know, and protect yourself by keeping the **door** open."

### Behavioral ‚Äî simplifying something too complex

So we had a data pipeline that had honestly grown into a monster. It was three **Spark** jobs, a custom scheduler someone wrote, and a small **Flink** job in the middle ‚Äî all just to move and clean one dataset. Every week something in that chain broke, and on-call hated it. So I sat down and drew the whole thing out. I realized most of the steps were doing tiny transformations that could all live in one place. What I did was replace the three jobs and the **Flink** piece with a single **Spark** job and a normal **Airflow** schedule, and I deleted the custom scheduler completely ‚Äî that was a few thousand lines gone. The new version did exactly the same thing but with way fewer moving parts. After that, the **pager** went quiet for that pipeline for months. What I learned is, deleting a system is sometimes the best feature you can ship ‚Äî fewer moving parts means fewer things that wake you up at night.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What made that pipeline such a **monster**?" **A ‚Äî** "Three **Spark** jobs, a custom **scheduler**, and a **Flink** piece in the middle ‚Äî all to move and clean one dataset."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **first thing** you did?" **A ‚Äî** "I drew the whole thing **out**, and saw most steps were tiny **transforms** that could live in one place."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of simplifying it?" **A ‚Äî** "Trusting it was safe to **delete** the custom scheduler ‚Äî that was a few thousand **lines** gone."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the improvement?" **A ‚Äî** "The **pager** went quiet for that pipeline for **months** ‚Äî same output, far fewer moving parts."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **lesson** you took from it?" **A ‚Äî** "**Deleting** a system is sometimes the best feature you can ship ‚Äî fewer parts, fewer things that wake you up."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a big cost improvement

So at one point our cloud bill for the **Spark** cluster was getting really high. My manager asked if anyone could look into it, and I took it. The first thing I did was actually measure where the money went. And it turned out one nightly batch job was using a giant cluster but sitting almost idle most of the time. It was waiting on a slow **shuffle** and reading way more data than it needed. So I did two things. First, I switched the input tables to **Iceberg** with proper **partitioning**, so the job only read the days it needed instead of scanning everything. Second, I moved that job onto **spot instances**, since it could safely retry if a node went away. Together that cut the cluster bill for that job by about forty percent ‚Äî real money every month. What I learned is: before you tune anything, you measure. Most of the cost was hiding in one job nobody had looked at.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was your **first** move on the cost problem?" **A ‚Äî** "I **measured** where the money went ‚Äî one nightly **batch** job ran a giant cluster but sat almost **idle**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of the work?" **A ‚Äî** "Finding the **waste** ‚Äî the cost was hiding in one job nobody had ever **looked** at."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you actually **change**?" **A ‚Äî** "Two things ‚Äî moved the inputs to **Iceberg** with proper **partitioning**, and ran the job on **spot instances**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why was **spot** safe for that job?" **A ‚Äî** "It could safely **retry** if a node went away, so we got the cheap price with no real **risk**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the savings?" **A ‚Äî** "We cut that job's **cluster** bill by about **forty percent** ‚Äî real money every month, just from measuring first."

### Behavioral ‚Äî a problem that only showed up under load

So we had a **JVM** service behind an API that worked perfectly in testing. But in production, only at peak traffic, the **p99 latency** would suddenly jump and some requests would time out. The hard part was that I could never reproduce it on my laptop ‚Äî I just couldn't make enough load. So what I did was set up a **load test** that hammered the service the same way real peak traffic did. And I turned on **continuous profiling** so I could see what the threads were doing during the slow moments. The profile showed it clearly. Under load, all the threads were stuck waiting on a **connection pool** to the database that was too small. Requests just queued up. The fix was to size the pool correctly and add a sensible **timeout**, so a slow query couldn't block everything. After that the **p99** stayed flat even at peak. What I learned is: some bugs only live under real load, so you have to recreate the load and watch the system ‚Äî not guess.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of that one?" **A ‚Äî** "I couldn't **reproduce** it on my laptop ‚Äî I just couldn't make enough **load** to trigger it."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you finally **measure** it?" **A ‚Äî** "I ran a **load test** at real peak and turned on **continuous profiling** to watch the **threads**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did the **profile** actually show?" **A ‚Äî** "Every **thread** was stuck waiting on a **connection pool** that was way too small, so requests just **queued**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What would you do **differently** next time?" **A ‚Äî** "I'd add a sensible **timeout** from day one, so one slow **query** can't block the whole service."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you know the **fix** worked?" **A ‚Äî** "After we sized the **pool** right, the **p99 latency** stayed **flat** even at full peak."

### Behavioral ‚Äî turning around a demanding data science team

So we had a **data science** team that was really unhappy with our **feature pipeline**. They needed fresh features every hour, but our **Spark** batch job only ran twice a day. And they were pretty loud about it in meetings ‚Äî kind of blaming the platform team for everything. At first I felt defensive. But then I just sat down with their lead for an hour and asked her to walk me through her actual day. And it turned out the real pain wasn't the schedule at all. It was that when a job failed, nobody told them. So they'd train models on stale data without knowing. What I did was add a simple **freshness** check and a **Slack** alert that pinged their channel directly, plus I moved the heaviest features to a more frequent run. After that the relationship completely changed. They went from complaining to actually inviting me to their planning. What I learned is: when a **stakeholder** is angry, the thing they're shouting about is usually not the real problem.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **turning point** with that team?" **A ‚Äî** "Sitting with their lead for an hour and asking her to walk me through her **actual day**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **real problem** under the noise?" **A ‚Äî** "Not the **schedule** ‚Äî when a job failed, nobody told them, so they trained on **stale** data."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **change** concretely?" **A ‚Äî** "A **freshness** check, a **Slack** alert straight to their channel, and the heavy features ran more often."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **relationship** end up?" **A ‚Äî** "They went from **complaining** in meetings to actually **inviting** me to their planning."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **take away** from it?" **A ‚Äî** "When a **stakeholder** is loud, the thing they're shouting is usually not the **real** problem."

### Behavioral ‚Äî hard feedback I got on my code reviews

To be honest, this one stung a bit. In a review with my manager, he told me my **code reviews** were slowing the whole team down. I thought I was being careful and thorough. But the feedback was that I left too many tiny **nitpick** comments ‚Äî naming, style, small things ‚Äî and people were waiting two or three days just to merge a small **Kafka** consumer change. My first reaction was, but I'm trying to keep quality high. I sat with it for a day, though, and I realized he was right. The cost was real. So what I did was start splitting my comments into two buckets: "this blocks merge" and "this is just my opinion, take it or leave it." And for the style stuff, I pushed us to add a **linter** so a machine catches it, not me. My review turnaround dropped to under a day. What I learned is: being thorough isn't free. There's always a **trade-off** with speed, and I wasn't seeing the speed side.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What exactly was the **feedback**?" **A ‚Äî** "My **code reviews** were slowing the team ‚Äî too many tiny **nitpick** comments on naming and style."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **react** at first?" **A ‚Äî** "Defensive. I thought, but I'm keeping **quality** high. I had to sit with it for a day."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **change**?" **A ‚Äî** "I split comments into **'blocks merge'** versus **'just my opinion'**, and pushed for a **linter**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** the improvement?" **A ‚Äî** "My review **turnaround** dropped from two or three days to under a **day**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **lesson** you carry now?" **A ‚Äî** "Being thorough isn't free ‚Äî there's always a **trade-off** with speed, and I wasn't seeing it."

### Behavioral ‚Äî giving hard feedback to a senior engineer

This was uncomfortable because the person was more senior than me. We were building a new **Trino** query layer, and he kept merging changes straight to the main branch with almost no tests. He trusted his own code. And he was good ‚Äî but a couple of his changes broke our **dashboards** in production. The **on-call** person that week was me, so I had to clean it up at night. I didn't want to call him out in a meeting and embarrass him. Instead I asked him for a coffee chat, and I framed it around me, not him. I said: "Hey, I was the one paged last night. I think if we had even one **integration test** on these queries, I would've slept." I kept it about the impact, not about him being careless. And he actually took it well. He said he hadn't thought about who was on the other end of his changes. After that he started writing tests. What I learned is: feedback lands better when you make it about the **shared cost**, not about the person.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "Why was that one so **uncomfortable**?" **A ‚Äî** "He was **more senior** than me, and genuinely good ‚Äî so calling it out felt risky."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the actual **impact** you raised?" **A ‚Äî** "His untested **Trino** changes broke **dashboards**, and I was the **on-call** cleaning it up at night."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **frame** it so it landed?" **A ‚Äî** "Over coffee, about **me** ‚Äî 'I was paged last night; one **integration test** and I'd have slept.'"
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did he **react**?" **A ‚Äî** "He took it well ‚Äî he said he'd never thought about who was on the **other end** of his changes."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you **learn** from it?" **A ‚Äî** "Feedback lands when you make it about the **shared cost**, not about the **person**."

### Behavioral ‚Äî helping a struggling teammate without making it worse

We had a junior engineer who joined the team. He was clearly drowning on his first real task ‚Äî adding a new sink to a **Flink** job. Weeks went by, and he kept saying "almost done" in standup. But the **pull request** never came. I could tell he was stuck and probably too embarrassed to ask. I didn't want to just take it over, because that would crush him. So what I did was ask him to pair with me on something unrelated first. I said I needed his help reading some logs. That got us talking with no pressure. And then I casually said, "Show me where you're at on the Flink thing." Within ten minutes I saw he was blocked on **checkpointing** config ‚Äî and honestly that confuses everyone. We solved it together in an afternoon, but I let him drive the keyboard so it stayed his work. He shipped it the next day. What I learned is: sometimes the kindest way to help is to lower the stakes first, so asking for help doesn't feel like failing.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **notice** he was stuck?" **A ‚Äî** "Weeks of 'almost done' in standup, but the **pull request** never came."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why not just **take it over**?" **A ‚Äî** "That would've **crushed** him. I didn't want asking for help to feel like **failing**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "So what did you **actually do**?" **A ‚Äî** "I asked him to **pair** with me on something unrelated first, so we'd talk with no **pressure**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was he really **blocked** on?" **A ‚Äî** "A **checkpointing** config on a **Flink** sink that honestly confuses everyone."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you keep it **his** win?" **A ‚Äî** "I let him **drive the keyboard** the whole time, so it stayed his work ‚Äî he shipped it next day."

### Behavioral ‚Äî explaining a cost trade-off to executives

So leadership wanted to know why our cloud bill kept growing. They were close to just telling us to "cut costs," which would've meant slowing down everything. I got pulled into a meeting with a couple of VPs who don't know what **Iceberg** or **Parquet** is, and I had maybe ten minutes. I knew if I talked about **compaction** or **file sizes** I'd lose them completely. So I used an analogy instead. I said: imagine a warehouse where we keep every receipt as its own tiny piece of paper. Finding anything means opening millions of drawers ‚Äî and that's what we pay for. Then I showed one simple **dashboard**: cost per query, before and after we batched the small files together. Just two bars. And I gave them a clear choice ‚Äî spend a bit on a cleanup job now, or keep paying more every month forever. They got it immediately and approved it on the spot. What I learned is: executives don't need the **architecture**. They need the decision framed as a simple **trade-off** with a number.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **risk** going into that meeting?" **A ‚Äî** "They were about to just say **'cut costs'**, which would've slowed everything down."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why not explain the **real** mechanism?" **A ‚Äî** "If I'd said **compaction** or **file sizes** to those **VPs**, I'd have lost them in a minute."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you do **instead**?" **A ‚Äî** "An **analogy** ‚Äî a warehouse keeping every receipt as its own tiny scrap of paper."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** it for them?" **A ‚Äî** "One **dashboard**, two bars ‚Äî **cost per query** before and after we batched the small files."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's the **takeaway**?" **A ‚Äî** "Executives don't need the **architecture** ‚Äî they need the decision as a simple **trade-off** with a number."

### Behavioral ‚Äî saying no to protect my team from burnout

There was a stretch where every other team was throwing requests at us because we owned the central **data platform**. New **connectors**, custom reports, one-off **backfills** ‚Äî it never stopped. And my two engineers were starting to work weekends to keep up. I could see burnout coming. I had to start saying no. But I didn't want to just be the team that blocks everyone. So what I did was make all the incoming requests visible. I put them on one shared board with a rough cost in days next to each. Then I took it to my manager and the requesting **stakeholders** and said: "We can do any of these, but not all. Here's the list ‚Äî you tell me the top three for this quarter." Suddenly it wasn't me saying no. It was them prioritizing. And about half the requests just quietly disappeared, because nobody really needed them. The weekend work stopped. What I learned is: the way to fight **scope creep** isn't to refuse ‚Äî it's to make the **trade-offs** visible so other people choose with you.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **warning sign**?" **A ‚Äî** "My two engineers were working **weekends** to keep up ‚Äî I could see **burnout** coming."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why not just **refuse** the requests?" **A ‚Äî** "I didn't want to be the team that **blocks** everyone ‚Äî that just makes you the enemy."
- **Q ‚Äî** "So what did you **do** instead?" **A ‚Äî** "I put every request on one **shared board** with a rough **cost in days** next to each."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did the **stakeholders** react?" **A ‚Äî** "It flipped ‚Äî instead of me saying no, **they** picked the top three for the quarter."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **result**?" **A ‚Äî** "Half the requests quietly **disappeared**, and the **weekend** work stopped."

### Behavioral ‚Äî tell me about myself

So I'm a **back-end** engineer, and over the last several years I've moved more and more into **big-data platform** work. I started out writing plain **JVM** services in Java ‚Äî APIs, some business logic. And what I found was, the parts I enjoyed most were always the hard ones underneath: why is this slow, why does this fall over at scale, how does the data actually flow. So I kept following that. Over time I ended up working on the **data platform** itself ‚Äî the **Kafka** streams, the **Spark** and **Flink** jobs, the **Iceberg** tables and **Trino** on top. Basically the **infrastructure** that other teams build their pipelines on. What I'm good at is connecting the low-level detail ‚Äî like a **garbage collection** pause or a **partition** count ‚Äî to the thing the business actually cares about, like whether the report is ready by morning. And honestly, what I enjoy most is the **multiplier** part. When I get the platform right, fifty other engineers move faster and don't even have to think about it. That's the kind of **impact** I like.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "What **pulled** you from backend into platform work?" **A ‚Äî** "The hard parts underneath ‚Äî why it's slow, why it **falls over** at scale, how the data flows."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What do you think you're genuinely **good at**?" **A ‚Äî** "Connecting a low-level thing ‚Äî a **GC pause** or a **partition** count ‚Äî to what the business cares about."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What part do you **enjoy** the most?" **A ‚Äî** "The **multiplier** part ‚Äî I get the **platform** right, and fifty engineers move faster without thinking about it."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Can you give a **concrete example** of that span?" **A ‚Äî** "Sure ‚Äî **Kafka** streams, **Spark** and **Flink** jobs, **Iceberg** tables, **Trino** on top, all as one platform."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What kind of **impact** drives you?" **A ‚Äî** "The kind other people **stand on** ‚Äî infrastructure they build pipelines on without even noticing."

### Behavioral ‚Äî why I want to move

So to be honest, it's not that anything is wrong where I am now. I've learned a lot and I like my team. The thing is, I've kind of grown into the edges of what the role can give me. At my current company the **data platform** is fairly settled, so most of my work now is keeping it running rather than building new things. What pulls me toward this role is the scope. You're working at a bigger scale, with harder **distributed systems** problems. And the platform is still being shaped ‚Äî so there's room to actually design and own things, not just maintain them. I want to be somewhere where the problems stretch me again, where I'm a bit uncomfortable in a good way. And I really like that here the **platform** work is taken seriously as its own thing, not treated as plumbing. So for me this is about growth and bigger **ownership** ‚Äî not about running away from anything.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "Is anything **wrong** where you are now?" **A ‚Äî** "No ‚Äî I've learned a lot and like my team. I've just grown into the **edges** of the role."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What specifically **pulls** you toward this role?" **A ‚Äî** "The **scope** ‚Äî bigger scale, harder **distributed systems**, and a platform still being **shaped**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What's **missing** in your current work?" **A ‚Äî** "Our **data platform** is settled, so it's mostly **keeping it running**, not building new things."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What kind of **growth** are you after?" **A ‚Äî** "I want to **design and own** things, and be a little **uncomfortable** in a good way again."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why **this** place in particular?" **A ‚Äî** "Here the **platform** work is taken seriously as its own thing, not treated as **plumbing**."

### Behavioral ‚Äî my greatest strength

So I think my biggest strength is that I dig until I actually understand the root cause, instead of stopping at the symptom. Let me give you a quick example. We had a **Spark** job that would randomly fail maybe one run in ten. The easy thing would've been to just add a retry and move on ‚Äî and a lot of people wanted to do that. But it bugged me that we didn't know why. So I spent a couple of days going through the **Spark UI** and the logs across the failed runs. And I found the pattern. The failures all happened when one upstream table got a late, oversized file, and that one **partition** blew past the memory limit. Once I knew that, the real fix was small ‚Äî and the job just stopped failing. So the strength isn't that I'm the smartest person in the room. It's that I'm stubborn about understanding the real cause before I patch something. In platform work that pays off a lot, because a hidden root cause usually comes back bigger later.

**Practice ‚Äî situational Q&A:**

- **Q ‚Äî** "Can you give a **concrete example** of that strength?" **A ‚Äî** "A **Spark** job failed one run in ten; everyone wanted a **retry**, but it bugged me we didn't know **why**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What was the **hardest part** of chasing it?" **A ‚Äî** "Two days in the **Spark UI** and logs, comparing failed runs to find the **pattern**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "What did you actually **find**?" **A ‚Äî** "An upstream table got a late **oversized** file, and that one **partition** blew past the memory limit."
- **Q ‚Äî** "How did you **measure** that it paid off?" **A ‚Äî** "The real **fix** was tiny, and the job simply **stopped failing**."
- **Q ‚Äî** "Why does that **matter** in platform work?" **A ‚Äî** "A hidden **root cause** usually comes back **bigger** later ‚Äî a **retry** would've just hidden it."

### Behavioral ‚Äî my greatest weakness

So my real weakness, honestly, is that I tend to keep things in my head instead of writing them down and sharing them early. When I'm deep in a problem ‚Äî say I'm redesigning how a **Kafka** topic is partitioned ‚Äî I'll figure the whole thing out on my own. And I'll only show people once it's basically done. The problem with that is, sometimes a teammate would've caught a flaw way earlier. Or they just feel left out of a decision that affects them. I noticed this was slowing the team down, not just me.
